style="white-space: nowrap;">Website</th><td class="infobox-data"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://microsoft.com/ie">microsoft<wbr />.com<wbr />/ie</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Internet Explorer</b><sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> (formerly <b>Microsoft Internet Explorer</b><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> and <b>Windows Internet Explorer</b>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> commonly abbreviated <b>IE</b> or <b>MSIE</b>) is a series of <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">graphical</a> <a href="/wiki/Web_browser" title="Web browser">web browsers</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> and included in the <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> line of <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating systems</a>, starting in 1995. It was first released as part of the add-on package <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Plus!" title="Microsoft Plus!">Plus! for Windows 95</a> that year. Later versions were available as free downloads, or in-<a href="/wiki/Service_pack" title="Service pack">service packs</a>, and included in the <a href="/wiki/Original_equipment_manufacturer" title="Original equipment manufacturer">original equipment manufacturer</a> (OEM) service releases of Windows 95 and later versions of Windows. New feature development for the browser was discontinued in 2016<sup id="cite_ref-is-ie-discontinued_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-is-ie-discontinued-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> in favor of new browser <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Edge" title="Microsoft Edge">Microsoft Edge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Since Internet Explorer is a Windows component and is included in long-term lifecycle versions of Windows such as <a href="/wiki/Windows_Server_2019" title="Windows Server 2019">Windows Server 2019</a>, it will continue to receive security updates until at least 2029.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Microsoft announced in August 2020 that as of August 2021,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> web-based <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_365" title="Microsoft 365">Microsoft 365</a> products will no longer support Internet Explorer, while <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Teams" title="Microsoft Teams">Microsoft Teams</a> ended support for IE earlier in November 2020.  \n</p><p>Internet Explorer was once the most widely used web browser, attaining a peak of about 95% <a href="/wiki/Usage_share_of_web_browsers" title="Usage share of web browsers">usage share</a> by 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> This came after Microsoft used <a href="/wiki/Product_bundling" title="Product bundling">bundling</a> to win the <a href="/wiki/First_browser_war" class="mw-redirect" title="First browser war">first browser war</a> against <a href="/wiki/Netscape" title="Netscape">Netscape</a>, which was the dominant browser in the 1990s. Its usage share has since declined with the launch of <a href="/wiki/Firefox" title="Firefox">Firefox</a> (2004) and <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a> (2008), and with the growing popularity of mobile operating systems such as <a href="/wiki/Android_(operating_system)" title="Android (operating system)">Android</a> and <a href="/wiki/IOS" title="IOS">iOS</a> that do not support Internet Explorer.\n</p><p>Estimates for Internet Explorer\'s market share in 2021 <a href="/wiki/Usage_share_of_web_browsers#Summary_table" title="Usage share of web browsers">are about 0.81% across all platforms</a>, or by StatCounter\'s numbers ranked 8th.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> On traditional PCs, the only platform on which it has ever had significant share, it is ranked 6th at 1.89%, after <a href="/wiki/Opera_(web_browser)" title="Opera (web browser)">Opera</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Edge" title="Microsoft Edge">Microsoft Edge</a>, IE\'s successor, first overtook Internet Explorer in terms of market share in November 2019. \n</p><p>Microsoft spent over <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$100 million</span> per year on Internet Explorer in the late 1990s,<sup id="cite_ref-victor_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-victor-15">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> with over 1,000 people involved in the project by 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-citeworld-ie-rise-fall_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-citeworld-ie-rise-fall-16">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-justice-gov-98-1232_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-justice-gov-98-1232-17">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Versions of Internet Explorer for other operating systems have also been produced, including an <a href="/wiki/Xbox_360" title="Xbox 360">Xbox 360</a> version called Internet Explorer for Xbox and for platforms Microsoft no longer supports: <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_for_Mac" class="mw-redirect" title="Internet Explorer for Mac">Internet Explorer for Mac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_for_UNIX" title="Internet Explorer for UNIX">Internet Explorer for UNIX</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solaris_(operating_system)" title="Solaris (operating system)">Solaris</a> and <a href="/wiki/HP-UX" title="HP-UX">HP-UX</a>), and an embedded OEM version called Pocket Internet Explorer, later rebranded <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_Mobile" title="Internet Explorer Mobile">Internet Explorer Mobile</a> made for <a href="/wiki/Windows_CE" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows CE">Windows CE</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windows_Phone" title="Windows Phone">Windows Phone</a>, and, previously, based on Internet Explorer 7, for <a href="/wiki/Windows_Mobile" title="Windows Mobile">Windows Phone 7</a>.\n</p><p>On March 17, 2015, Microsoft announced that <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Edge" title="Microsoft Edge">Microsoft Edge</a> would replace Internet Explorer as the default browser on its <a href="/wiki/Windows_10" title="Windows 10">Windows 10</a> devices. This effectively makes <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_11" title="Internet Explorer 11">Internet Explorer 11</a> the last release. Internet Explorer, however, remains on Windows 10 and <a href="/wiki/Windows_Server_2019" title="Windows Server 2019">Windows Server 2019</a> primarily for enterprise purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Since January 12, 2016, only Internet Explorer 11 has official support for consumers; extended support for Internet Explorer 10 ended on January 31, 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Support varies based on the operating system\'s technical capabilities and its support life cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-IELifecycle_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IELifecycle-23">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The browser has been scrutinized throughout its development for use of third-party technology (such as the <a href="/wiki/Source_code" title="Source code">source code</a> of <a href="/wiki/Spyglass_Mosaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Spyglass Mosaic">Spyglass Mosaic</a>, used without royalty in early versions) and security and privacy <a href="/wiki/Vulnerability_(computing)" title="Vulnerability (computing)">vulnerabilities</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Microsoft" class="mw-redirect" title="United States v. Microsoft">the United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/European_Union_Microsoft_competition_case#Related_investigations" class="mw-redirect" title="European Union Microsoft competition case">the European Union have alleged</a> that integration of Internet Explorer with Windows has been to the detriment of fair browser competition.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc" role="navigation" aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Extensibility"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Extensibility</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Security"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Security</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Security_vulnerabilities"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Security vulnerabilities</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Vulnerability_exploited_in_attacks_on_U.S._firms"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Vulnerability exploited in attacks on U.S. firms</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Major_vulnerability_across_versions"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Major vulnerability across versions</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Internet_Explorer" title="History of Internet Explorer">History of Internet Explorer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_version_history" title="Internet Explorer version history">Internet Explorer version history</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internet_Explorer_1">Internet Explorer 1</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Internet Explorer 1">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Internet_Explorer_1.0.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/39/Internet_Explorer_1.0.png/220px-Internet_Explorer_1.0.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/39/Internet_Explorer_1.0.png/330px-Internet_Explorer_1.0.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/39/Internet_Explorer_1.0.png 2x" data-file-width="365" data-file-height="275" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Internet_Explorer_1.0.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Internet Explorer 1</div></div></div>\n<p>The Internet Explorer project was started in the summer of 1994 by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Reardon" title="Thomas Reardon">Thomas Reardon</a>, who, according to the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> Review of 2003,<sup id="cite_ref-tr35_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tr35-25">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> used source code from <a href="/wiki/Spyglass,_Inc." title="Spyglass, Inc.">Spyglass, Inc.</a> Mosaic, which was an early commercial web browser with formal ties to the pioneering <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Supercomputing_Applications" title="National Center for Supercomputing Applications">National Center for Supercomputing Applications</a> (NCSA) <a href="/wiki/Mosaic_(web_browser)" title="Mosaic (web browser)">Mosaic</a> browser.<sup id="cite_ref-bw_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw-26">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-win_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-win-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In late 1994, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> licensed Spyglass Mosaic for a quarterly fee plus a percentage of Microsoft\'s non-Windows revenues for the software.<sup id="cite_ref-win_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-win-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Although bearing a name like NCSA Mosaic, Spyglass Mosaic had used the NCSA Mosaic source code sparingly.<sup id="cite_ref-ericsink1_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ericsink1-28">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first version, dubbed Microsoft Internet Explorer, was installed as part of the <i>Internet Jumpstart Kit</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Plus!" title="Microsoft Plus!">Microsoft Plus!</a> pack for <a href="/wiki/Windows_95" title="Windows 95">Windows 95</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mshistory_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mshistory-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The Internet Explorer team began with about six people in early development.<sup id="cite_ref-ericsink1_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ericsink1-28">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Internet Explorer 1.5 was released several months later for <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT" title="Windows NT">Windows NT</a> and added support for basic table rendering. By including it free of charge with their <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a>, they did not have to pay royalties to Spyglass Inc, resulting in a lawsuit and a <a href="/wiki/United_States_dollar" title="United States dollar">US$</a>8&#160;million settlement on January 22, 1997.<sup id="cite_ref-bw_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bw-26">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Microsoft was sued by Synet Inc. in 1996, for trademark <a href="/wiki/Infringement_procedure" class="mw-redirect" title="Infringement procedure">infringement</a>, claiming it owned the rights to the name "Internet Explorer".<sup id="cite_ref-Zdnet.co.uk_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zdnet.co.uk-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="Was that lawsuit successful? Was it thrown out as frivolous? Was some settlement reached? (November 2020)">needs update</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Internet_Explorer_2.E2.80.9310"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Internet_Explorer_2\xe2\x80\x9310">Internet Explorer 2\xe2\x80\x9310</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Internet Explorer 2\xe2\x80\x9310">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_2" title="Internet Explorer 2">Internet Explorer 2</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_3" title="Internet Explorer 3">Internet Explorer 3</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_4" title="Internet Explorer 4">Internet Explorer 4</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_5" title="Internet Explorer 5">Internet Explorer 5</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_6" title="Internet Explorer 6">Internet Explorer 6</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_7" title="Internet Explorer 7">Internet Explorer 7</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_8" title="Internet Explorer 8">Internet Explorer 8</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_9" title="Internet Explorer 9">Internet Explorer 9</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_10" title="Internet Explorer 10">Internet Explorer 10</a></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Internet_Explorer_11">Internet Explorer 11</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Internet Explorer 11">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_11" title="Internet Explorer 11">Internet Explorer 11</a></div>\n<p>Internet Explorer 11 is featured in <a href="/wiki/Windows_8.1" title="Windows 8.1">Windows 8.1</a>, which was released on October 17, 2013. It includes an incomplete mechanism for syncing tabs. It is a major update to its <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_Developer_Tools" title="Internet Explorer Developer Tools">developer tools</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ie11-7-preview_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ie11-7-preview-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> enhanced scaling for high DPI screens,<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/HTML5" title="HTML5">HTML5</a> prerender and prefetch,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hardware-accelerated" class="mw-redirect" title="Hardware-accelerated">hardware-accelerated</a> <a href="/wiki/JPEG" title="JPEG">JPEG</a> decoding,<sup id="cite_ref-PCWord_Bradley_Business_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCWord_Bradley_Business-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Closed_captioning" title="Closed captioning">closed captioning</a>, HTML5 full screen,<sup id="cite_ref-ghack_Brinkmann_Preview_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ghack_Brinkmann_Preview-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> and is the first Internet Explorer to support <a href="/wiki/WebGL" title="WebGL">WebGL</a><sup id="cite_ref-cnet-ie11dev_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cnet-ie11dev-39">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-verge-ie11webgl_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verge-ie11webgl-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> and Google\'s protocol <a href="/wiki/SPDY" title="SPDY">SPDY</a> (starting at v3).<sup id="cite_ref-techcrunch-spdy_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-techcrunch-spdy-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> This version of IE has features dedicated to Windows 8.1, including cryptography (WebCrypto),<sup id="cite_ref-ie11-7-preview_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ie11-7-preview-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_bitrate_streaming" title="Adaptive bitrate streaming">adaptive bitrate streaming</a> (<a href="/wiki/Media_Source_Extensions" title="Media Source Extensions">Media Source Extensions</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-BetaNews_Williams_DevPreview_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BetaNews_Williams_DevPreview-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Encrypted_Media_Extensions" title="Encrypted Media Extensions">Encrypted Media Extensions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ghack_Brinkmann_Preview_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ghack_Brinkmann_Preview-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Internet Explorer 11 was made available for <a href="/wiki/Windows_7" title="Windows 7">Windows 7</a> users to download on November 7, 2013, with <a href="/wiki/Windows_Update#Automatic_Updates" title="Windows Update">Automatic Updates</a> in the following weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-ieblog-ie11-w7_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ieblog-ie11-w7-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Internet Explorer 11\'s <a href="/wiki/User_agent" title="User agent">user agent</a> string now identifies the agent as "<a href="/wiki/Trident_(layout_engine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trident (layout engine)">Trident</a>" (the underlying layout engine) instead of "MSIE.\xe2\x80\x9d It also announces compatibility with <a href="/wiki/Gecko_(layout_engine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gecko (layout engine)">Gecko</a> (the layout engine of <a href="/wiki/Firefox" title="Firefox">Firefox</a>).\n</p><p>Microsoft claimed that Internet Explorer 11, running the <a href="/wiki/WebKit" title="WebKit">WebKit</a> SunSpider <a href="/wiki/JavaScript" title="JavaScript">JavaScript</a> Benchmark, was the fastest browser as of October 15, 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Internet Explorer 11 was made available for <a href="/wiki/Windows_Server_2012" title="Windows Server 2012">Windows Server 2012</a> and <a href="/wiki/Windows_Embedded_8_Standard" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows Embedded 8 Standard">Windows Embedded 8 Standard</a> in the spring of 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-Win8IE11_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Win8IE11-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_life">End of life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: End of life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Edge" title="Microsoft Edge">Microsoft Edge</a>, officially unveiled on January 21, 2015, has replaced Internet Explorer as the default browser on <a href="/wiki/Windows_10" title="Windows 10">Windows 10</a>. Internet Explorer is still installed in Windows 10 to maintain compatibility with older websites and <a href="/wiki/Intranet" title="Intranet">intranet</a> sites that require <a href="/wiki/ActiveX" title="ActiveX">ActiveX</a> and other Microsoft legacy web technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-ieblog-spartan_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ieblog-spartan-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ieblog-edge_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ieblog-edge-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-verge-spartanextensions_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-verge-spartanextensions-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to Microsoft, the development of new features for Internet Explorer has ceased. However, it will continue to be <a href="/wiki/Software_maintenance" title="Software maintenance">maintained</a> as part of the support policy for the versions of Windows with which it is included.<sup id="cite_ref-is-ie-discontinued_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-is-ie-discontinued-7">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>On June 1, 2020, the <a href="/wiki/Internet_Archive" title="Internet Archive">Internet Archive</a> removed the latest version of Internet Explorer from its list of supported browsers, citing its dated infrastructure that makes it hard to work with, following the suggestion of Microsoft Chief of Security Chris Jackson that users not use it as their default browser, but to use it only for websites that require it.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>From November 30, 2020, the web version of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Teams" title="Microsoft Teams">Microsoft Teams</a> can no longer be accessed using Internet Explorer 11. The remaining O365 applications will no longer support IE11 on August 17, 2021.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The browser itself will continue to be supported for the lifecycle of the Windows version on which it is installed.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Internet_Explorer_zoom_menu.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Internet_Explorer_zoom_menu.png/220px-Internet_Explorer_zoom_menu.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Internet_Explorer_zoom_menu.png/330px-Internet_Explorer_zoom_menu.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/92/Internet_Explorer_zoom_menu.png 2x" data-file-width="429" data-file-height="299" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Internet_Explorer_zoom_menu.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Page_zoom" class="mw-redirect" title="Page zoom">Page zoom</a> as seen in <a href="/wiki/IE9" class="mw-redirect" title="IE9">IE9</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Internet Explorer has been designed to view a broad range of web pages and provide certain features within the operating system, including <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Update" class="mw-redirect" title="Microsoft Update">Microsoft Update</a>. During the heyday of the <a href="/wiki/Browser_wars" title="Browser wars">browser wars</a>, Internet Explorer superseded <a href="/wiki/Netscape" title="Netscape">Netscape</a> only when it caught up technologically to support the progressive features of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (January 2013)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Standards_support">Standards support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Standards support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Internet Explorer, using the Trident <a href="/wiki/Browser_engine" title="Browser engine">layout engine</a>:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Supports <a href="/wiki/HTML" title="HTML">HTML</a> 4.01, parts of HTML5, <a href="/wiki/Cascading_Style_Sheets" class="mw-redirect" title="Cascading Style Sheets">CSS</a> Level 1, Level 2, and Level 3, <a href="/wiki/XML" title="XML">XML</a> 1.0, and <a href="/wiki/Document_Object_Model" title="Document Object Model">DOM</a> Level 1, with minor implementation gaps.</li>\n<li>Fully supports <a href="/wiki/XSLT" title="XSLT">XSLT</a> 1.0 as well as an obsolete Microsoft dialect of XSLT often referred to as <i>WD-xsl</i>, which was loosely based on the December 1998 W3C Working Draft of <a href="/wiki/Extensible_Stylesheet_Language" class="mw-redirect" title="Extensible Stylesheet Language">XSL</a>. Support for <a href="/wiki/XSLT_2.0" class="mw-redirect" title="XSLT 2.0">XSLT 2.0</a> lies in the future: semi-official Microsoft bloggers have indicated that development is underway, but no dates have been announced.</li>\n<li>Almost full conformance to CSS 2.1 has been added in the Internet Explorer 8 release.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The trident rendering engine in Internet Explorer 9 in 2011, scored highest in the official W3C conformance test suite for CSS 2.1 of all major browsers.</li>\n<li>Supports <a href="/wiki/XHTML" title="XHTML">XHTML</a> in Internet Explorer 9 (Trident version 5.0). Prior versions can render XHTML documents authored with HTML compatibility principles and served with a <code>text/html</code> <a href="/wiki/MIME" title="MIME">MIME-type</a>.</li>\n<li>Supports a subset<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> of <a href="/wiki/Scalable_Vector_Graphics" title="Scalable Vector Graphics">SVG</a> in Internet Explorer 9 (Trident version 5.0), excluding SMIL, SVG fonts and filters.</li></ul>\n<p>Internet Explorer uses <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_layout_engines_(HTML)#DOCTYPE_sniffing" class="mw-redirect" title="Comparison of layout engines (HTML)">DOCTYPE sniffing</a> to choose between standards mode and a "<a href="/wiki/Quirks_mode" title="Quirks mode">quirks mode</a>" in which it deliberately mimics nonstandard behaviors of old versions of MSIE for HTML and CSS rendering on screen (Internet Explorer always uses standards mode for printing). It also provides its own dialect of <a href="/wiki/ECMAScript" title="ECMAScript">ECMAScript</a> called <a href="/wiki/JScript" title="JScript">JScript</a>.\n</p><p>Internet Explorer was criticized by <a href="/wiki/Tim_Berners-Lee" title="Tim Berners-Lee">Tim Berners-Lee</a> for its limited support for SVG, which is promoted by <a href="/wiki/W3C" class="mw-redirect" title="W3C">W3C</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-TBLcrit_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TBLcrit-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-standard_extensions">Non-standard extensions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Non-standard extensions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Internet Explorer has introduced an array of proprietary extensions to many of the standards, including HTML, CSS, and the DOM. This has resulted in several web pages that appear broken in standards-compliant web browsers and has introduced the need for a "quirks mode" to allow for rendering improper elements meant for Internet Explorer in these other browsers.\n</p><p>Internet Explorer has introduced several extensions to the DOM that have been adopted by other browsers. \n</p><p>These include the inner HTML property, which provides access to the HTML string within an element, which was part of IE 5 and was standardized as part of HTML 5 roughly 15 years later after all other browsers implemented it for compatibility,<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> the XMLHttpRequest object, which allows the sending of HTTP request and receiving of HTTP response, and may be used to perform <a href="/wiki/AJAX" class="mw-redirect" title="AJAX">AJAX</a>, and the designMode attribute of the content Document object, which enables rich text editing of HTML documents.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Some of these functionalities were not possible until the introduction of the W3C DOM methods. Its <a href="/wiki/Ruby_character" title="Ruby character">Ruby character</a> extension to HTML is also accepted as a module in W3C XHTML 1.1, though it is not found in all versions of W3C HTML.\n</p><p>Microsoft submitted several other features of IE for consideration by the W3C for standardization. These include the \'behavior\' CSS property, which connects the HTML elements with JScript behaviors (known as HTML Components, HTC), <a href="/wiki/HTMLplusTIME" class="mw-redirect" title="HTMLplusTIME">HTML+TIME</a> profile, which adds timing and media synchronization support to HTML documents (similar to the W3C <a href="/wiki/XHTMLplusSMIL" class="mw-redirect" title="XHTMLplusSMIL">XHTML+SMIL</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/VML" class="mw-redirect" title="VML">VML</a> <a href="/wiki/Vector_graphics" title="Vector graphics">vector graphics</a> file format. However, all were rejected, at least in their original forms; VML was subsequently combined with <a href="/wiki/PGML" class="mw-redirect" title="PGML">PGML</a> (proposed by <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Adobe Systems">Adobe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sun_Microsystems" title="Sun Microsystems">Sun</a>), resulting in the W3C-approved SVG format, one of the few vector image formats being used on the web, which IE did not support until version 9.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other non-standard behaviors include: support for vertical text, but in a syntax different from W3C CSS3 candidate recommendation, support for a variety of image effects<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> and page transitions, which are not found in W3C CSS, support for <a href="/wiki/Obfuscated_code" class="mw-redirect" title="Obfuscated code">obfuscated</a> script code, in particular <a href="/wiki/JScript.Encode" title="JScript.Encode">JScript.Encode</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> as well as support for <a href="/wiki/Font_embedding" title="Font embedding">embedding</a> <a href="/wiki/Embedded_OpenType" title="Embedded OpenType">EOT</a> fonts in <a href="/wiki/Font_embedding_on_the_Web" class="mw-redirect" title="Font embedding on the Web">web pages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Favicon">Favicon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Favicon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Support for <a href="/wiki/Favicon" title="Favicon">favicons</a> was first added in Internet Explorer 5.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Internet Explorer supports favicons in <a href="/wiki/Portable_Network_Graphics" title="Portable Network Graphics">PNG</a>, static <a href="/wiki/GIF" title="GIF">GIF</a> and <a href="/wiki/ICO_(file_format)" title="ICO (file format)">native Windows icon</a> formats. In Windows Vista and later, Internet Explorer can display native Windows icons that have embedded PNG files.<sup id="cite_ref-jeffdavis_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jeffdavis-66">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ericlaw-1_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ericlaw-1-67">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Usability_and_accessibility">Usability and accessibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Usability and accessibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Internet Explorer makes use of the accessibility framework provided in Windows. Internet Explorer is also a user interface for FTP, with operations similar to Windows Explorer. <a href="/wiki/Pop-up_blocking" class="mw-redirect" title="Pop-up blocking">Pop-up blocking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tabbed_browsing" class="mw-redirect" title="Tabbed browsing">tabbed browsing</a> were added respectively in Internet Explorer 6 and Internet Explorer 7. Tabbed browsing can also be added to older versions by installing <a href="/wiki/MSN_Search_Toolbar" class="mw-redirect" title="MSN Search Toolbar">MSN Search Toolbar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Yahoo_Toolbar" class="mw-redirect" title="Yahoo Toolbar">Yahoo Toolbar</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cache">Cache</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Cache">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Temporary_Internet_Files" title="Temporary Internet Files">Temporary Internet Files</a> and <a href="/wiki/Index.dat" title="Index.dat">Index.dat</a></div>\n<p>Internet Explorer caches visited content in the <a href="/wiki/Temporary_Internet_Files" title="Temporary Internet Files">Temporary Internet Files</a> folder to allow quicker access (or offline access) to previously visited pages. The content is indexed in a database file, known as <a href="/wiki/Index.dat" title="Index.dat">Index.dat</a>. Multiple Index.dat files exist which index different content\xe2\x80\x94visited content, <a href="/wiki/Web_feed" title="Web feed">web feeds</a>, visited <a href="/wiki/URL" title="URL">URLs</a>, cookies, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prior to IE7, clearing the cache used to clear the index but the files themselves were not reliably removed, posing a potential security and privacy risk. In IE7 and later, when the cache is cleared, the cache files are more reliably removed, and the index.dat file is overwritten with null bytes.\n</p><p>Caching has been improved in IE9.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Group_Policy">Group Policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Group Policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Group_Policy" title="Group Policy">Group Policy</a></div>\n<p>Internet Explorer is fully configurable using <a href="/wiki/Group_Policy" title="Group Policy">Group Policy</a>. Administrators of <a href="/wiki/Windows_Server_domain" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows Server domain">Windows Server domains</a> (for domain-joined computers) or the local computer can apply and enforce a variety of settings on computers that affect the user interface (such as disabling menu items and individual configuration options), as well as underlying security features such as downloading of files, zone configuration, per-site settings, ActiveX control behavior and others. Policy settings can be configured for each user and for each machine. Internet Explorer also supports <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Windows_Authentication" title="Integrated Windows Authentication">Integrated Windows Authentication</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:IExplore.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/IExplore.svg/220px-IExplore.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/IExplore.svg/330px-IExplore.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/IExplore.svg/440px-IExplore.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="706" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:IExplore.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The architecture of IE8. Previous versions had a similar architecture, except that both tabs and the UI were within the same process. Consequently, each browser window could have only one "tab process".</div></div></div>\n<p>Internet Explorer uses a <a href="/wiki/Software_componentry" class="mw-redirect" title="Software componentry">componentized</a> architecture built on the <a href="/wiki/Component_Object_Model" title="Component Object Model">Component Object Model</a> (COM) technology. It consists of several major components, each of which is contained in a separate <a href="/wiki/Dynamic-link_library" title="Dynamic-link library">dynamic-link library</a> (DLL) and exposes a set of COM <a href="/wiki/Interface_(computing)" title="Interface (computing)">programming interfaces</a> hosted by the Internet Explorer main executable, <samp style="padding-left:0.4em; padding-right:0.4em; color:#666666;">iexplore.exe</samp>:<sup id="cite_ref-architecture_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-architecture-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><samp style="padding-left:0.4em; padding-right:0.4em; color:#666666;">WinInet.dll</samp> is the protocol handler for <a href="/wiki/HTTP" class="mw-redirect" title="HTTP">HTTP</a>, <a href="/wiki/HTTPS" title="HTTPS">HTTPS</a>, and <a href="/wiki/FTP" class="mw-redirect" title="FTP">FTP</a>. It handles all network communication over these protocols.</li>\n<li><samp style="padding-left:0.4em; padding-right:0.4em; color:#666666;">URLMon.dll</samp> is responsible for MIME-type handling and download of web content, and provides a thread-safe wrapper around WinInet.dll and other protocol implementations.</li>\n<li><samp style="padding-left:0.4em; padding-right:0.4em; color:#666666;">MSHTML.dll</samp> houses the <a href="/wiki/Trident_(rendering_engine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trident (rendering engine)">Trident</a> <a href="/wiki/Rendering_(computer_graphics)" title="Rendering (computer graphics)">rendering engine</a> introduced in Internet Explorer 4, which is responsible for displaying the pages on-screen and handling the <a href="/wiki/Document_Object_Model" title="Document Object Model">Document Object Model</a> (DOM) of the web pages. MSHTML.dll parses the HTML/CSS file and creates the internal DOM tree representation of it. It also exposes a set of <a href="/wiki/API" title="API">APIs</a> for runtime inspection and modification of the DOM tree. The DOM tree is further processed by a layout engine which then renders the internal representation on screen.</li>\n<li><samp style="padding-left:0.4em; padding-right:0.4em; color:#666666;">IEFrame.dll</samp> contains the user interface and window of IE in Internet Explorer 7 and above.</li>\n<li><samp style="padding-left:0.4em; padding-right:0.4em; color:#666666;">ShDocVw.dll</samp> provides the navigation, local caching and history functionalities for the browser.</li>\n<li><samp style="padding-left:0.4em; padding-right:0.4em; color:#666666;">BrowseUI.dll</samp> is responsible for rendering the browser user interface such as menus and toolbars.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Internet Explorer does not include any native scripting functionality. Rather, <samp style="padding-left:0.4em; padding-right:0.4em; color:#666666;">MSHTML.dll</samp> exposes an API that permits a programmer to develop a scripting environment to be plugged-in and to access the DOM tree. Internet Explorer 8 includes the bindings for the <a href="/wiki/Active_Scripting" title="Active Scripting">Active Scripting</a> engine, which is a part of <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> and allows any language implemented as an Active Scripting module to be used for client-side scripting. By default, only the JScript and <a href="/wiki/VBScript" title="VBScript">VBScript</a> modules are provided; third party implementations like <a href="/wiki/ScreamingMonkey" class="mw-redirect" title="ScreamingMonkey">ScreamingMonkey</a> (for ECMAScript 4 support) can also be used. Microsoft also makes available the <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Silverlight" title="Microsoft Silverlight">Microsoft Silverlight</a> runtime that allows <a href="/wiki/CLI_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="CLI languages">CLI languages</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_Language_Runtime" title="Dynamic Language Runtime">DLR</a>-based dynamic languages like <a href="/wiki/IronPython" title="IronPython">IronPython</a> and <a href="/wiki/IronRuby" title="IronRuby">IronRuby</a>, to be used for client-side scripting.\n</p><p>Internet Explorer 8 introduced some major architectural changes, called <i>loosely coupled IE</i> (LCIE). LCIE separates the main window process (frame process) from the processes hosting the different web applications in different tabs (tab processes). A frame process can create multiple tab processes, each of which can be of a different <a href="/wiki/Mandatory_Integrity_Control" title="Mandatory Integrity Control">integrity level</a>, each tab process can host multiple web sites. The processes use asynchronous <a href="/wiki/Inter-process_communication" title="Inter-process communication">inter-process communication</a> to synchronize themselves. Generally, there will be a single frame process for all web sites. In <a href="/wiki/Windows_Vista" title="Windows Vista">Windows Vista</a> with protected mode turned on, however, opening privileged content (such as local HTML pages) will create a new tab process as it will not be constrained by protected mode.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extensibility">Extensibility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Extensibility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Internet Explorer exposes a set of Component Object Model (COM) interfaces that allows <a href="/wiki/Plug-in_(computing)" title="Plug-in (computing)">add-ons</a> to extend the functionality of the browser.<sup id="cite_ref-architecture_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-architecture-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Extensibility is divided into two types: Browser extensibility and content extensibility. Browser extensibility involves adding context menu entries, toolbars, menu items or <a href="/wiki/Browser_Helper_Objects" class="mw-redirect" title="Browser Helper Objects">Browser Helper Objects</a> (BHO). BHOs are used to extend the feature set of the browser, whereas the other extensibility options are used to expose that feature in the user interface. Content extensibility adds support for non-native content formats.<sup id="cite_ref-architecture_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-architecture-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> It allows Internet Explorer to handle new <a href="/wiki/File_format" title="File format">file formats</a> and new <a href="/wiki/Network_protocol" class="mw-redirect" title="Network protocol">protocols</a>, e.g. <a href="/wiki/WebM" title="WebM">WebM</a> or SPDY.<sup id="cite_ref-architecture_70-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-architecture-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In addition, web pages can integrate <a href="/wiki/Software_widget" title="Software widget">widgets</a> known as ActiveX controls which run on Windows only but have vast potentials to extend the content capabilities; <a href="/wiki/Adobe_Flash_Player" title="Adobe Flash Player">Adobe Flash Player</a> and Microsoft Silverlight are examples.<sup id="cite_ref-architecture_70-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-architecture-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Add-ons can be installed either locally, or directly by a web site.\n</p><p>Since malicious add-ons can compromise the security of a system, Internet Explorer implements several safeguards. Internet Explorer 6 with Service Pack 2 and later feature an Add-on Manager for enabling or disabling individual add-ons, complemented by a "No Add-Ons" mode. <a href="/wiki/Security_and_safety_features_new_to_Windows_Vista#Application_isolation" title="Security and safety features new to Windows Vista">Starting with Windows Vista</a>, Internet Explorer and its BHOs run with restricted <a href="/wiki/Privileges_and_Immunities_Clause" title="Privileges and Immunities Clause">privileges</a> and are isolated from the rest of the system. Internet Explorer 9 introduced a new component&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 Add-on Performance Advisor. Add-on Performance Advisor shows a notification when one or more of installed add-ons exceed a pre-set performance threshold. The notification appears in the Notification Bar when the user launches the browser. Windows 8 and Windows RT introduce a <a href="/wiki/Metro-style_app" class="mw-redirect" title="Metro-style app">Metro-style version</a> of Internet Explorer that is entirely sandboxed and does not run add-ons at all.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In addition, Windows RT cannot download or install ActiveX controls at all; although existing ones bundled with Windows RT still run in the traditional version of Internet Explorer.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-73">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Internet Explorer itself can be hosted by other applications via a set of COM interfaces. This can be used to embed the browser functionality inside a computer program or create <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_shell" title="Internet Explorer shell">Internet Explorer shells</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-architecture_70-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-architecture-70">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Security">Security</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Security">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Browser_security" title="Browser security">Browser security</a></div>\n<p>Internet Explorer uses a zone-based <a href="/wiki/Computer_security" title="Computer security">security</a> framework that groups sites based on certain conditions, including whether it is an Internet- or intranet-based site as well as a user-editable whitelist. Security restrictions are applied per zone; all the sites in a zone are subject to the restrictions.\n</p><p>Internet Explorer 6 SP2 onwards uses the <i>Attachment Execution Service</i> of Microsoft Windows to mark executable files downloaded from the Internet as being potentially unsafe. Accessing files marked as such will prompt the user to make an explicit trust decision to execute the file, as executables originating from the Internet can be potentially unsafe. This helps in preventing the accidental installation of malware.\n</p><p>Internet Explorer 7 introduced the phishing filter, which restricts access to <a href="/wiki/Phishing" title="Phishing">phishing</a> sites unless the user overrides the decision. With version 8, it also blocks access to sites known to host <a href="/wiki/Malware" title="Malware">malware</a>. Downloads are also checked to see if they are known to be malware-infected.\n</p><p>In Windows Vista, Internet Explorer by default runs in what is called <i>Protected Mode</i>, where the privileges of the browser itself are severely restricted\xe2\x80\x94it cannot make any system-wide changes. One can optionally turn this mode off, but this is not recommended. This also effectively restricts the privileges of any add-ons. As a result, even if the browser or any add-on is compromised, the damage the security breach can cause is limited.\n</p><p>Patches and updates to the browser are released periodically and made available through the Windows Update service, as well as through Automatic Updates. Although security patches continue to be released for a range of platforms, most feature additions and security infrastructure improvements are only made available on operating systems that are in Microsoft\'s mainstream support phase.\n</p><p>On December 16, 2008, <a href="/wiki/Trend_Micro" title="Trend Micro">Trend Micro</a> recommended users switch to rival browsers until an emergency patch was released to fix a potential security risk which "could allow outside users to take control of a person\'s computer and steal their passwords.\xe2\x80\x9d Microsoft representatives countered this recommendation, claiming that "0.02% of internet sites" were affected by the flaw. A fix for the issue was released the following day with the Security Update for Internet Explorer KB960714, on Microsoft Windows Update.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2010, Germany\'s Federal Office for Information Security, known by its German initials, BSI, advised "temporary use of alternative browsers" because of a "critical security hole" in Microsoft\'s software that could allow hackers to remotely plant and run malicious code on Windows PCs.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2011, a report by Accuvant, funded by Google, rated the security (based on sandboxing) of Internet Explorer worse than <a href="/wiki/Google_Chrome" title="Google Chrome">Google Chrome</a> but better than <a href="/wiki/Mozilla_Firefox" class="mw-redirect" title="Mozilla Firefox">Mozilla Firefox</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A 2017 browser security white paper comparing Google Chrome, Microsoft Edge, and <a href="/wiki/Internet_Explorer_11" title="Internet Explorer 11">Internet Explorer 11</a> by X41 D-Sec in 2017 came to similar conclusions, also based on sandboxing and support of legacy web technologies.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Security_vulnerabilities">Security vulnerabilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Security vulnerabilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Comparison_of_web_browsers#Security_and_vulnerabilities" title="Comparison of web browsers">Comparison of web browsers \xc2\xa7&#160;Security and vulnerabilities</a></div>\n<p>Internet Explorer has been subjected to many security vulnerabilities and concerns such that the volume of criticism for IE is unusually high. Much of the <a href="/wiki/Spyware" title="Spyware">spyware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adware" title="Adware">adware</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Computer_virus" title="Computer virus">computer viruses</a> across the Internet are made possible by exploitable bugs and flaws in the security architecture of Internet Explorer, sometimes requiring nothing more than viewing of a malicious web page to install themselves. This is known as a "<a href="/wiki/Drive-by_download" title="Drive-by download">drive-by install</a>.\xe2\x80\x9d There are also attempts to trick the user into installing malicious software by misrepresenting the software\'s true purpose in the description section of an ActiveX security alert.\n</p><p>A number of security flaws affecting IE originated not in the browser itself, but in ActiveX-based add-ons used by it. Because the add-ons have the same privilege as IE, the flaws can be as critical as browser flaws. This has led to the ActiveX-based architecture being criticized for being fault-prone. By 2005, some experts maintained that the dangers of ActiveX had been overstated and there were safeguards in place.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In 2006, new techniques using <a href="/wiki/Automated_testing" class="mw-redirect" title="Automated testing">automated testing</a> found more than a hundred vulnerabilities in standard Microsoft ActiveX components.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> Security features introduced in Internet Explorer 7 mitigated some of these vulnerabilities.\n</p><p>In 2008, Internet Explorer had a number of published security vulnerabilities. According to research done by security research firm <a href="/wiki/Secunia" class="mw-redirect" title="Secunia">Secunia</a>, Microsoft did not respond as quickly as its competitors in fixing security holes and making patches available.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The firm also reported 366 vulnerabilities in ActiveX controls, an increase from the previous year.\n</p><p>According to an October 2010 report in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Register" title="The Register">The Register</a></i>, researcher Chris Evans had detected a known security vulnerability which, then dating back to 2008, had not been fixed for at least six hundred days.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Microsoft says that it had known about this vulnerability, but it was of exceptionally low severity as the victim web site must be configured in a peculiar way for this attack to be feasible at all.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In December 2010, researchers were able to bypass the "Protected Mode" feature in Internet Explorer.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vulnerability_exploited_in_attacks_on_U.S._firms">Vulnerability exploited in attacks on U.S. firms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Vulnerability exploited in attacks on U.S. firms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Operation_Aurora" title="Operation Aurora">Operation Aurora</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:StatCounter-browser-ww-monthly-201909-201909-map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/StatCounter-browser-ww-monthly-201909-201909-map.png/220px-StatCounter-browser-ww-monthly-201909-201909-map.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/StatCounter-browser-ww-monthly-201909-201909-map.png/330px-StatCounter-browser-ww-monthly-201909-201909-map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/StatCounter-browser-ww-monthly-201909-201909-map.png/440px-StatCounter-browser-ww-monthly-201909-201909-map.png 2x" data-file-width="1116" data-file-height="645" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:StatCounter-browser-ww-monthly-201909-201909-map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The most used web browser per country in 2020<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In an advisory on January 14, 2010, Microsoft said that attackers targeting Google and other U.S. companies used software that exploits a security hole, which had already been patched, in Internet Explorer. The vulnerability affected Internet Explorer 6 from on Windows XP and Server 2003, IE6 SP1 on Windows 2000 SP4, IE7 on Windows Vista, XP, Server 2008, and Server 2003, IE8 on Windows 7, Vista, XP, Server 2003, and Server 2008 (R2).<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German government</a> warned users against using Internet Explorer and recommended switching to an alternative web browser, due to the major security hole described above that was <a href="/wiki/Operation_Aurora#Attack_analysis" title="Operation Aurora">exploited in Internet Explorer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The Australian and French Government issued a similar warning a few days later.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_vulnerability_across_versions">Major vulnerability across versions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Major vulnerability across versions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On April 26, 2014, Microsoft issued a security advisory relating to <a href="/wiki/CVE_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="CVE (identifier)">CVE</a>-<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r999302996">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .citation q{quotes:"\\"""\\"""\'""\'"}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-free a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-free a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Lock-green.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output .id-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .id-lock-registration a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-registration a,.mw-parser-output .citation .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:linear-gradient(transparent,transparent),url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg")right 0.1em center/9px no-repeat}.mw-parser-output code.cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:none;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-hidden-error{display:none;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-visible-error{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-maint{display:none;color:#33aa33;margin-left:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .citation .mw-selflink{font-weight:inherit}</style><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://cve.mitre.org/cgi-bin/cvename.cgi?name=CVE-2014-1776">2014-1776</a> (use-after-free vulnerability in Microsoft Internet Explorer 6 through 11<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>), a vulnerability that could allow "remote code execution" in Internet Explorer versions 6 to 11.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> On April 28, 2014, the United States <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Homeland_Security" class="mw-redirect" title="Department of Homeland Security">Department of Homeland Security</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/United_States_Computer_Emergency_Readiness_Team" title="United States Computer Emergency Readiness Team">United States Computer Emergency Readiness Team</a> (US-CERT) released an advisory stating that the vulnerability could result in "the complete compromise" of an affected system.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> US-CERT recommended reviewing Microsoft\'s suggestions to mitigate an attack or using an alternate browser until the bug is fixed.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The UK National Computer Emergency Response Team (CERT-UK) published an advisory announcing similar concerns and for users to take the additional step of ensuring their antivirus software is up to date.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/NortonLifeLock" title="NortonLifeLock">Symantec</a>, a cyber security firm, confirmed that "the vulnerability crashes Internet Explorer on Windows XP".<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> The vulnerability was resolved on May 1, 2014, with a security update.<sup id="cite_ref-IEfix_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IEfix-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_adoption_and_usage_share">Market adoption and usage share</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Market adoption and usage share">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Internet_Explorer#Market_adoption_and_usage_share" title="History of Internet Explorer">History of Internet Explorer \xc2\xa7&#160;Market adoption and usage share</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Usage_share_of_web_browsers" title="Usage share of web browsers">Usage share of web browsers</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Usage_share_of_web_browsers_(Source_StatCounter).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Usage_share_of_web_browsers_%28Source_StatCounter%29.svg/220px-Usage_share_of_web_browsers_%28Source_StatCounter%29.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Usage_share_of_web_browsers_%28Source_StatCounter%29.svg/330px-Usage_share_of_web_browsers_%28Source_StatCounter%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Usage_share_of_web_browsers_%28Source_StatCounter%29.svg/440px-Usage_share_of_web_browsers_%28Source_StatCounter%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1260" data-file-height="630" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Usage_share_of_web_browsers_(Source_StatCounter).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Usage_share_of_web_browsers" title="Usage share of web browsers">Usage share of web browsers</a> according to <a href="/wiki/StatCounter" title="StatCounter">StatCounter</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Internet-explorer-usage-data.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Internet-explorer-usage-data.svg/300px-Internet-explorer-usage-data.svg.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Internet-explorer-usage-data.svg/450px-Internet-explorer-usage-data.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Internet-explorer-usage-data.svg/600px-Internet-explorer-usage-data.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="587" data-file-height="290" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Internet-explorer-usage-data.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Historical market share of Internet Explorer</div></div></div>\n<p>The adoption rate of Internet Explorer seems to be closely related to that of Microsoft Windows, as it is the default web browser that comes with Windows. Since the integration of Internet Explorer 2.0 with Windows 95 OSR 1 in 1996, and especially after version 4.0\'s release in 1997, the adoption was greatly accelerated: from below 20% in 1996, to about 40% in 1998, and over 80% in 2000. This made Microsoft the winner in the infamous \'<a href="/wiki/First_browser_war" class="mw-redirect" title="First browser war">first browser war</a>\' against Netscape. <a href="/wiki/Netscape_Navigator" title="Netscape Navigator">Netscape Navigator</a> was the dominant browser during 1995 and until 1997, but rapidly lost share to IE starting in 1998, and eventually slipped behind in 1999. The integration of IE with Windows led to a lawsuit by <a href="/wiki/AOL" title="AOL">AOL</a>, Netscape\'s owner, accusing Microsoft of unfair competition. The infamous case was eventually won by AOL but by then it was too late, as Internet Explorer had already become the dominant browser.\n</p><p>Internet Explorer peaked during 2002 and 2003, with about 95% share. Its first notable competitor after beating Netscape was Firefox from <a href="/wiki/Mozilla" title="Mozilla">Mozilla</a>, which itself was an offshoot from Netscape.\n</p><p>Firefox 1.0 had surpassed Internet Explorer 5 in early 2005, with Firefox 1.0 at 8 percent market share.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Approximate usage over time based on various usage share counters averaged for the year overall, or for the fourth quarter, or for the last month in the year depending on availability of reference.<sup id="cite_ref-nashare_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nashare-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-zdnetearly_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zdnetearly-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-thecountermonth_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thecountermonth-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thecounter.com_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thecounter.com-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bhn_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bhn-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-wbs_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wbs-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to StatCounter Internet Explorer\'s market share fell below 50% in September 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> In May 2012, Google Chrome overtook Internet Explorer as the most used browser worldwide, according to StatCounter.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry_adoption">Industry adoption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Industry adoption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Browser_Helper_Object" title="Browser Helper Object">Browser Helper Objects</a> are also used by many <a href="/wiki/Web_search_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Web search engine">search engines</a> companies and third parties for creating add-ons that access their services, such as search engine toolbars. Because of the use of COM, it is possible to embed web-browsing functionality in third-party applications. Hence, there are several Internet Explorer shells, and several content-centric applications like <a href="/wiki/RealPlayer" title="RealPlayer">RealPlayer</a> also use Internet Explorer\'s web browsing module for viewing web pages within the applications.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Removal">Removal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Removal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Removal_of_Internet_Explorer" title="Removal of Internet Explorer">Removal of Internet Explorer</a></div>\n<p>While a major upgrade of Internet Explorer can be uninstalled in a traditional way if the user has saved the original application files for installation, the matter of uninstalling the version of the browser that has shipped with an operating system remains a controversial one.\n</p><p>The idea of removing a stock install of Internet Explorer from a Windows system was proposed during the <i><a href="/wiki/United_States_v._Microsoft_Corp._(2001)" class="mw-redirect" title="United States v. Microsoft Corp. (2001)">United States v. Microsoft Corp.</a></i> case. One of Microsoft\'s arguments during the trial was that removing Internet Explorer from Windows may result in system instability. Indeed, programs that depend on libraries installed by IE, including Windows help and support system, fail to function without IE. Before Windows Vista, it was not possible to run <a href="/wiki/Windows_Update" title="Windows Update">Windows Update</a> without IE because the service used ActiveX technology, which no other web browser supports.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impersonation_by_malware">Impersonation by malware</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Impersonation by malware">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The popularity of Internet Explorer has led to the appearance of malware abusing its name. On January 28, 2011, a fake Internet Explorer browser calling itself "Internet Explorer \xe2\x80\x93 Emergency Mode" appeared. It closely resembles the real Internet Explorer but has fewer buttons and no search bar. If a user attempts to launch any other browser such as Google Chrome, Mozilla Firefox, <a href="/wiki/Opera_(web_browser)" title="Opera (web browser)">Opera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Safari_(web_browser)" title="Safari (web browser)">Safari</a>, or the real Internet Explorer, this browser will be loaded instead. It also displays a fake error message, claiming that the computer is infected with malware and Internet Explorer has entered "Emergency Mode.\xe2\x80\x9d It blocks access to legitimate sites such as Google if the user tries to access them.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Internet_Explorer&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<style
